</p><p>Charlemagne instituted principles for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accountancy" title="Accountancy">accounting practice</a> by means of the <a href="/wiki/Capitulare_de_villis" title="Capitulare de villis">Capitulare de villis</a> of 802, which laid down strict rules for the way in which incomes and expenses were to be recorded.
</p><p>Charlemagne applied this system to much of the European continent, and Offa's standard was voluntarily adopted by much of England. After Charlemagne's death, continental coinage degraded, and most of Europe resorted to using the continued high-quality English coin until about 1100.
</p>
<h3><span id="Jews_in_Charlemagne.27s_realm"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jews_in_Charlemagne's_realm">Jews in Charlemagne's realm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Jews in Charlemagne's realm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early in Charlemagne's rule he tacitly allowed Jews to monopolise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Money_lending" title="Money lending">money lending</a>. The lending of money in return for <a href="/wiki/Interest" title="Interest">interest</a> was proscribed in 814 because it violated Church law. Charlemagne introduced the <i><a href="/wiki/Capitulary_for_the_Jews" title="Capitulary for the Jews">Capitulary for the Jews</a></i>, a prohibition on Jews engaging in money-lending due to the religious convictions of the majority of his constituents. Effectively banning money lending was a reversal of his earlier recorded general policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[130]</a></sup> Charlemagne also performed a significant number of microeconomic reforms, such as direct control of <a href="/wiki/Price" title="Price">prices</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feudal_levies" title="Feudal levies">feudal levies</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He invited Italian Jews to immigrate, as royal clients independent of the feudal landowners, and form trading communities in the agricultural regions of <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a>. Their trading activities augmented the otherwise almost exclusively agricultural economies of these regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Charlemagne's <i>Capitulary for the Jews</i> was not representative of his overall economic relationship or attitude towards the Frankish Jews; this relationship evolved throughout his reign. His personal <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a>, for example, was Jewish,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[132]</a></sup> and he employed one Jew, Isaac, who was his personal representative to the Muslim caliphate of Baghdad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_reforms">Education reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Education reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charlemagne_c_800.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="457" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Charlemagne_c_800.jpg/170px-Charlemagne_c_800.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Charlemagne_c_800.jpg/255px-Charlemagne_c_800.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Charlemagne_c_800.jpg/340px-Charlemagne_c_800.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charlemagne_c_800.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Charlemagne in a contemporary sketch</div></div></div>
<p>Part of Charlemagne's success as a warrior, an administrator and ruler can be traced to his admiration for learning and education. His reign is often referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a> because of the flowering of scholarship, literature, art and architecture that characterise it. Charlemagne came into contact with the culture and learning of other countries (especially Moorish Spain, Anglo-Saxon England,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[134]</a></sup> and Lombard Italy) due to his vast conquests. He greatly increased the provision of monastic schools and scriptoria (centres for book-copying) in Francia.
</p><p>Charlemagne was a lover of books, sometimes having them read to him during meals. He was thought to enjoy the works of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-147">[135]</a></sup> His court played a key role in producing books that taught elementary Latin and different aspects of the church. It also played a part in creating a royal library that contained in-depth works on language and Christian faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Charlemagne encouraged clerics to translate Christian creeds and prayers into their respective vernaculars as well to teach grammar and music. Due to the increased interest of intellectual pursuits and the urging of their king, the monks accomplished so much copying that almost every manuscript from that time was preserved. At the same time, at the urging of their king, scholars were producing more secular books on many subjects, including history, poetry, art, music, law, theology, etc. Due to the increased number of titles, private libraries flourished. These were mainly supported by aristocrats and churchmen who could afford to sustain them. At Charlemagne's court, a library was founded and a number of copies of books were produced, to be distributed by Charlemagne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson2013_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2013-29">[21]</a></sup> Book production was completed slowly by hand and took place mainly in large monastic libraries. Books were so in demand during Charlemagne's time that these libraries lent out some books, but only if that borrower offered valuable collateral in return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson2013_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2013-29">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Privilegio_di_carlo_magno_alla_chiesa_di_modena,_782_(facsimile).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1640" data-file-width="2924" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Privilegio_di_carlo_magno_alla_chiesa_di_modena%2C_782_%28facsimile%29.JPG/220px-Privilegio_di_carlo_magno_alla_chiesa_di_modena%2C_782_%28facsimile%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Privilegio_di_carlo_magno_alla_chiesa_di_modena%2C_782_%28facsimile%29.JPG/330px-Privilegio_di_carlo_magno_alla_chiesa_di_modena%2C_782_%28facsimile%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Privilegio_di_carlo_magno_alla_chiesa_di_modena%2C_782_%28facsimile%29.JPG/440px-Privilegio_di_carlo_magno_alla_chiesa_di_modena%2C_782_%28facsimile%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Privilegio_di_carlo_magno_alla_chiesa_di_modena,_782_(facsimile).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The privileges of Charlemagne at the <a href="/wiki/Modena_Cathedral" title="Modena Cathedral">Modena Cathedral</a> (containing the <a href="/wiki/Monogram" title="Monogram">monogram</a> of Charlemagne), dated 782</div></div></div>
<p>Most of the surviving works of classical Latin were copied and preserved by Carolingian scholars. Indeed, the earliest manuscripts available for many ancient texts are Carolingian. It is almost certain that a text which survived to the Carolingian age survives still.
</p><p>The pan-European nature of Charlemagne's influence is indicated by the origins of many of the men who worked for him: <a href="/wiki/Alcuin" title="Alcuin">Alcuin</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> from <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodulf" title="Theodulf">Theodulf</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigoth</a>, probably from <a href="/wiki/Septimania" title="Septimania">Septimania</a>; <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Deacon" title="Paul the Deacon">Paul the Deacon</a>, Lombard; <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italians</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_of_Pisa" title="Peter of Pisa">Peter of Pisa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Paulinus_II" title="Saint Paulinus II">Paulinus of Aquileia</a>; and Franks <a href="/wiki/Angilbert" title="Angilbert">Angilbert</a>, Angilram, <a href="/wiki/Einhard" title="Einhard">Einhard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waldo_of_Reichenau" title="Waldo of Reichenau">Waldo of Reichenau</a>.
</p><p>Charlemagne promoted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal_arts" title="Liberal arts">liberal arts</a> at court, ordering that his children and grandchildren be well-educated, and even studying himself (in a time when even leaders who promoted education did not take time to learn themselves) under the tutelage of Peter of Pisa, from whom he learned grammar; Alcuin, with whom he studied rhetoric, dialectic (logic), and astronomy (he was particularly interested in the movements of the stars); and Einhard, who tutored him in arithmetic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDutton2016_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDutton2016-150">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>His great scholarly failure, as Einhard relates, was his inability to write: when in his old age he attempted to learn—practising the formation of letters in his bed during his free time on books and wax tablets he hid under his pillow—"his effort came too late in life and achieved little success", and his ability to read—which Einhard is silent about, and which no contemporary source supports—has also been called into question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDutton2016_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDutton2016-150">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 800, Charlemagne enlarged the hostel at the <a href="/wiki/Muristan" title="Muristan">Muristan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> and added a library to it. He certainly had not been personally in Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_reforms">Church reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Church reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne_and_church_music" title="Charlemagne and church music">Charlemagne and church music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aachen_Cathedral_top_floor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="1050" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Aachen_Cathedral_top_floor.jpg/170px-Aachen_Cathedral_top_floor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Aachen_Cathedral_top_floor.jpg/255px-Aachen_Cathedral_top_floor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Aachen_Cathedral_top_floor.jpg/340px-Aachen_Cathedral_top_floor.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aachen_Cathedral_top_floor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palatine_Chapel_in_Aachen" title="Palatine Chapel in Aachen">Charlemagne's chapel</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aachen_Cathedral" title="Aachen Cathedral">Aachen Cathedral</a></div></div></div>
<p>Charlemagne expanded the reform Church's programme unlike his father, Pippin, and uncle, Carloman. The deepening of the spiritual life was later to be seen as central to public policy and royal governance. His reform focused on strengthening the church's power structure, improving clergy's skill and moral quality, standardising liturgical practices, improvements on the basic tenets of the faith and the rooting out of paganism. His authority extended over church and state. He could discipline clerics, control ecclesiastical property and define orthodox doctrine. Despite the harsh legislation and sudden change, he had developed support from clergy who approved his desire to deepen the piety and morals of his subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-153">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 809–810, Charlemagne called a church council in <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a>, which confirmed the unanimous belief in the West that the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father and the Son (<i>ex Patre <a href="/wiki/Filioque" title="Filioque">Filioque</a></i>) and sanctioned inclusion in the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a> of the phrase <i>Filioque</i> (and the Son). For this Charlemagne sought the approval of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a>. The Pope, while affirming the doctrine and approving its use in teaching, opposed its inclusion in the text of the Creed as adopted in the 381 <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">First Council of Constantinople</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[142]</a></sup> This spoke of the procession of the Holy Spirit from the Father, without adding phrases such as "and the Son", "through the Son", or "alone". Stressing his opposition, the Pope had the original text inscribed in Greek and Latin on two heavy shields that were displayed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Peter%27s_Basilica" title="Saint Peter's Basilica">Saint Peter's Basilica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_reforms">Writing reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Writing reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Codexaureus_04.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="730" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Codexaureus_04.jpg/170px-Codexaureus_04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Codexaureus_04.jpg/255px-Codexaureus_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Codexaureus_04.jpg/340px-Codexaureus_04.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Codexaureus_04.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorsch_Gospels" title="Lorsch Gospels">Lorsch Gospels</a> of Charlemagne's reign</div></div></div>
<p>During Charles' reign, the <a href="/wiki/Uncial_script" title="Uncial script">Roman half uncial</a> script and its cursive version, which had given rise to various continental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minuscule_cursive" title="Minuscule cursive">minuscule</a> scripts, were combined with features from the <a href="/wiki/Insular_script" title="Insular script">insular scripts</a> in use in Irish and English monasteries. <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_minuscule" title="Carolingian minuscule">Carolingian minuscule</a> was created partly under the patronage of Charlemagne. <a href="/wiki/Alcuin" title="Alcuin">Alcuin</a>, who ran the palace school and <a href="/wiki/Scriptorium" title="Scriptorium">scriptorium</a> at Aachen, was probably a chief influence.
</p><p>The revolutionary character of the Carolingian reform, however, can be overemphasised; efforts at taming Merovingian and Germanic influence had been underway before Alcuin arrived at Aachen. The new minuscule was disseminated first from Aachen and later from the influential scriptorium at <a href="/wiki/Tours" title="Tours">Tours</a>, where Alcuin retired as an abbot.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_reforms">Political reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Political reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Charlemagne engaged in many reforms of Frankish governance while continuing many traditional practices, such as the division of the kingdom among sons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Divisio_regnorum">Divisio regnorum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Divisio regnorum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 806, Charlemagne first made provision for the traditional division of the empire on his death. For Charles the Younger he designated Austrasia and Neustria, Saxony, Burgundy and <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringia</a>. To Pippin, he gave Italy, Bavaria, and <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">Swabia</a>. Louis received Aquitaine, the Spanish March and <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a>. The imperial title was not mentioned, which led to the suggestion that, at that particular time, Charlemagne regarded the title as an honorary achievement that held no hereditary significance.
</p><p>Pepin died in 810 and Charles in 811. Charlemagne then reconsidered the matter, and in 813, crowned his youngest son, Louis, co-emperor and co-King of the Franks, granting him a half-share of the empire and the rest upon Charlemagne's own death. The only part of the Empire that Louis was not promised was Italy, which Charlemagne specifically bestowed upon Pippin's illegitimate son <a href="/wiki/Bernard_of_Italy" title="Bernard of Italy">Bernard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appearance">Appearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Appearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manner">Manner</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Manner">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:StrasbourgCath_BasCoteN_09.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/StrasbourgCath_BasCoteN_09.JPG/220px-StrasbourgCath_BasCoteN_09.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/StrasbourgCath_BasCoteN_09.JPG/330px-StrasbourgCath_BasCoteN_09.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/StrasbourgCath_BasCoteN_09.JPG/440px-StrasbourgCath_BasCoteN_09.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:StrasbourgCath_BasCoteN_09.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>13th-century stained glass depiction of Charlemagne, <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg_Cathedral" title="Strasbourg Cathedral">Strasbourg Cathedral</a></div></div></div><p>
Einhard tells in his twenty-fourth chapter: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Charles was temperate in eating, and particularly so in drinking, for he abominated drunkenness in anybody, much more in himself and those of his household; but he could not easily abstain from food, and often complained that fasts injured his health. He very rarely gave entertainments, only on great feast-days, and then to large numbers of people. His meals ordinarily consisted of four courses, not counting the roast, which his huntsmen used to bring in on the spit; he was more fond of this than of any other dish. While at table, he listened to reading or music. The subjects of the readings were the stories and deeds of olden time: he was fond, too, of St. Augustine's books, and especially of the one titled "<a href="/wiki/The_City_of_God" title="The City of God">The City of God</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEinhard1880ch._24._Habits_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEinhard1880ch._24._Habits-160">[148]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Charlemagne threw grand banquets and feasts for special occasions such as religious holidays and four of his weddings. When he was not working, he loved Christian books, horseback riding, swimming, bathing in natural hot springs with his friends and family, and hunting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhote_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bhote-161">[149]</a></sup> Franks were well known for horsemanship and hunting skills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhote_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bhote-161">[149]</a></sup> Charles was a light sleeper and would stay in his bed chambers for entire days at a time due to restless nights. During these days, he would not get out of bed when a quarrel occurred in his kingdom, instead summoning all members of the situation into his bedroom to be given orders. Einhard tells again in the twenty-fourth chapter: "In summer after the midday meal, he would eat some fruit, drain a single cup, put off his clothes and shoes, just as he did for the night, and rest for two or three hours. He was in the habit of awaking and rising from bed four or five times during the night."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhote_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bhote-161">[149]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Theodiscus" title="Theodiscus">Theodiscus</a></div>
<p>Charlemagne probably spoke a Rhenish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franconian_languages" title="Franconian languages">Franconian</a> dialect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChambersWilkie2014_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChambersWilkie2014-163">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKitterick2008318_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKitterick2008318-164">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>He also spoke <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and had at least some understanding of Greek, according to Einhard (<i>Grecam vero melius intellegere quam pronuntiare poterat</i>, "he could understand Greek better than he could speak it").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEinhard1880ch._25._Studies_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEinhard1880ch._25._Studies-165">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largely fictional account of Charlemagne's Iberian campaigns by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudo-Turpin" title="Pseudo-Turpin">Pseudo-Turpin</a>, written some three centuries after his death, gave rise to the legend that the king also spoke <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herwaarden2003_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herwaarden2003-166">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Physical appearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Marville,_H%C3%B4tel_Carnavalet,_statue_of_Charlemagne,_ca._1853%E2%80%9370.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6809" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Charles_Marville%2C_H%C3%B4tel_Carnavalet%2C_statue_of_Charlemagne%2C_ca._1853%E2%80%9370.jpg/170px-Charles_Marville%2C_H%C3%B4tel_Carnavalet%2C_statue_of_Charlemagne%2C_ca._1853%E2%80%9370.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Charles_Marville%2C_H%C3%B4tel_Carnavalet%2C_statue_of_Charlemagne%2C_ca._1853%E2%80%9370.jpg/255px-Charles_Marville%2C_H%C3%B4tel_Carnavalet%2C_statue_of_Charlemagne%2C_ca._1853%E2%80%9370.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Charles_Marville%2C_H%C3%B4tel_Carnavalet%2C_statue_of_Charlemagne%2C_ca._1853%E2%80%9370.jpg/340px-Charles_Marville%2C_H%C3%B4tel_Carnavalet%2C_statue_of_Charlemagne%2C_ca._1853%E2%80%9370.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Marville,_H%C3%B4tel_Carnavalet,_statue_of_Charlemagne,_ca._1853%E2%80%9370.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Carolingian-era <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_statuette_of_Charlemagne" title="Equestrian statuette of Charlemagne">equestrian statuette</a> thought to represent Charlemagne (from <a href="/wiki/Metz_Cathedral" title="Metz Cathedral">Metz Cathedral</a>, now in the Louvre)</div></div></div>
<p>Charlemagne's personal appearance is known from a good description by Einhard after his death in the biography <i><a href="/wiki/Vita_Karoli_Magni" title="Vita Karoli Magni">Vita Karoli Magni</a></i>. Einhard states:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2004116_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2004116-167">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>He was heavily built, sturdy, and of considerable stature, although not exceptionally so, since his height was seven times the length of his own foot. He had a round head, large and lively eyes, a slightly larger nose than usual, white but still attractive hair, a bright and cheerful expression, a short and fat neck, and he enjoyed good health, except for the fevers that affected him in the last few years of his life. Towards the end, he dragged one leg. Even then, he stubbornly did what he wanted and refused to listen to doctors, indeed he detested them, because they wanted to persuade him to stop eating roast meat, as was his wont, and to be content with boiled meat.</p></blockquote>
<p>The physical portrait provided by Einhard is confirmed by contemporary depictions such as coins and his 8-inch (20 cm) <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_statuette_of_Charlemagne" title="Equestrian statuette of Charlemagne">bronze statuette</a> kept in the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a>. In 1861, Charlemagne's tomb was opened by scientists who reconstructed his skeleton and estimated it to be measured 1.95 metres (6 ft 5 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbero2004118_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbero2004118-168">[156]</a></sup> A 2010 estimate of his height from an <a href="/wiki/Radiology" title="Radiology">X-ray</a> and <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scan</a> of his <a href="/wiki/Tibia" title="Tibia">tibia</a> was 1.84 metres (6 ft 0 in). This puts him in the 99th <a href="/wiki/Percentile" title="Percentile">percentile</a> of height for his period, given that average male height of his time was 1.69 metres (5 ft 7 in). The width of the bone suggested he was <a href="/wiki/Gracility" title="Gracility">gracile</a> in body build.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dress">Dress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Dress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Ier_le_Grand_ou_Charlemagne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="670" data-file-width="469" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Charles_Ier_le_Grand_ou_Charlemagne.jpg/170px-Charles_Ier_le_Grand_ou_Charlemagne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Charles_Ier_le_Grand_ou_Charlemagne.jpg/255px-Charles_Ier_le_Grand_ou_Charlemagne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Charles_Ier_le_Grand_ou_Charlemagne.jpg/340px-Charles_Ier_le_Grand_ou_Charlemagne.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Ier_le_Grand_ou_Charlemagne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Later depiction of Charlemagne in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_Nationale_de_France" title="Bibliothèque Nationale de France">Bibliothèque Nationale de France</a></div></div></div>
<p>Charlemagne wore the traditional <a href="/wiki/Early_medieval_European_dress" title="Early medieval European dress">costume of the Frankish people</a>, described by Einhard thus:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEinhard1880ch._23._Dress_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEinhard1880ch._23._Dress-170">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>He used to wear the national, that is to say, the Frank, dress—next his skin a linen shirt and linen breeches, and above these a tunic fringed with silk; while hose fastened by bands covered his lower limbs, and shoes his feet, and he protected his shoulders and chest in winter by a close-fitting coat of otter or marten skins.</p></blockquote>
<p>He wore a blue cloak and always carried a sword typically of a golden or silver hilt. He wore intricately jeweled swords to banquets or ambassadorial receptions. Nevertheless:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEinhard1880ch._23._Dress_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEinhard1880ch._23._Dress-170">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>He despised foreign costumes, however handsome, and never allowed himself to be robed in them, except twice in Rome, when he donned the Roman tunic, <a href="/wiki/Chlamys" title="Chlamys">chlamys</a>, and shoes; the first time at the request of Pope Hadrian, the second to gratify Leo, Hadrian's successor.</p></blockquote>
<p>On great feast days, he wore embroidery and jewels on his clothing and shoes. He had a golden buckle for his cloak on such occasions and would appear with his great <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diadem_(personal_wear)" title="Diadem (personal wear)">diadem</a>, but he despised such apparel according to Einhard, and usually dressed like the common people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEinhard1880ch._23._Dress_170-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEinhard1880ch._23._Dress-170">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homes">Homes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Homes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Charlemagne had residences across his kingdom, including numerous private estates that were governed in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Capitulare_de_villis" title="Capitulare de villis">Capitulare de villis</a>. A 9th-century document detailing the inventory of an estate at Asnapium listed amounts of livestock, plants and vegetables and kitchenware including cauldrons, drinking cups, brass kettles and firewood. The manor contained seventeen houses built inside the courtyard for nobles and family members and was separated from its supporting villas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beatification">Beatification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Beatification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Charlemagne was revered as a saint in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> and some other locations after the twelfth century. The <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Apostolic See</a> did not recognise his invalid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canonisation" title="Canonisation">canonisation</a> by <a href="/wiki/Antipope_Paschal_III" title="Antipope Paschal III">Antipope Paschal III</a>, done to gain the favour of <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Barbarossa" title="Frederick Barbarossa">Frederick Barbarossa</a> in 1165. The Apostolic See annulled all of Paschal's ordinances at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Lateran_Council" title="Third Lateran Council">Third Lateran Council</a> in 1179.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[160]</a></sup> He is not enumerated among the 28 saints named "Charles" in the <i><a href="/wiki/Roman_Martyrology" title="Roman Martyrology">Roman Martyrology</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[161]</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Beatification" title="Beatification">beatification</a> has been acknowledged as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_process_of_beatification_and_canonization#Confirmation_of_cult" title="Historical process of beatification and canonization">cultus confirmed</a></i> and is celebrated on 28 January.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shahan_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-shahan-108">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoche_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoche-174">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">Cultural impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Cultural impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Iconography_of_Charlemagne" title="Iconography of Charlemagne">Iconography of Charlemagne</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The author of the <i><a href="/wiki/Visio_Karoli_Magni" title="Visio Karoli Magni">Visio Karoli Magni</a></i> written around 865 uses facts gathered apparently from Einhard and his own observations on the decline of Charlemagne's family after the dissensions war (840–43) as the basis for a visionary tale of Charles' meeting with a prophetic spectre in a dream.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jesus-College-MS-111_00181_91r_(cropped)_Ystoria_Carolo_Magno_-_Chronicl_Turpin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="844" data-file-width="789" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Jesus-College-MS-111_00181_91r_%28cropped%29_Ystoria_Carolo_Magno_-_Chronicl_Turpin.jpg/220px-Jesus-College-MS-111_00181_91r_%28cropped%29_Ystoria_Carolo_Magno_-_Chronicl_Turpin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Jesus-College-MS-111_00181_91r_%28cropped%29_Ystoria_Carolo_Magno_-_Chronicl_Turpin.jpg/330px-Jesus-College-MS-111_00181_91r_%28cropped%29_Ystoria_Carolo_Magno_-_Chronicl_Turpin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Jesus-College-MS-111_00181_91r_%28cropped%29_Ystoria_Carolo_Magno_-_Chronicl_Turpin.jpg/440px-Jesus-College-MS-111_00181_91r_%28cropped%29_Ystoria_Carolo_Magno_-_Chronicl_Turpin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jesus-College-MS-111_00181_91r_(cropped)_Ystoria_Carolo_Magno_-_Chronicl_Turpin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of a chain of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Welsh" title="Middle Welsh">Middle Welsh</a> legends about Charlemagne: <i>Ystorya de Carolo Magno</i> from the <i><a href="/wiki/Red_Book_of_Hergest" title="Red Book of Hergest">Red Book of Hergest</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Jesus_College,_Oxford" title="Jesus College, Oxford">Jesus College, Oxford</a>, MS 111), 14th century</div></div></div>
<p>Charlemagne was a model <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knight</a> as one of the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Worthies" title="Nine Worthies">Nine Worthies</a> who enjoyed an important legacy in European culture. One of the great medieval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_cycle" title="Literary cycle">literary cycles</a>, the Charlemagne cycle or the <i><a href="/wiki/Matter_of_France" title="Matter of France">Matter of France</a></i>, centres on his deeds—the Emperor with the Flowing Beard of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_of_Roland" title="Song of Roland">Roland</a></i> fame—and his historical commander of the border with <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roland" title="Roland">Roland</a>, and the 12 <a href="/wiki/Paladin" title="Paladin">paladins</a>. These are analogous to, and inspired the myth of, the <a href="/wiki/Knights_of_the_Round_Table" title="Knights of the Round Table">Knights of the Round Table</a> of <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>'s court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[163]</a></sup> Their tales constitute the first <i><a href="/wiki/Chanson_de_geste" title="Chanson de geste">chansons de geste</a></i>.
</p><p>In the 12th century, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Monmouth" title="Geoffrey of Monmouth">Geoffrey of Monmouth</a> based his stories of Arthur largely on stories of Charlemagne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[164]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a> in the 14th century, there was considerable cultural conflict in England, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_dynasty" title="Norman dynasty">Norman</a> rulers were aware of their French roots and identified with Charlemagne, Anglo-Saxon natives felt more affinity for Arthur, whose own legends were relatively primitive. Therefore, storytellers in England adapted legends of Charlemagne and his 12 Peers to the Arthurian tales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i>, the spirit of Charlemagne appears to <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Paradiso_(Dante)#Fifth_Sphere_(Mars:_The_Warriors_of_the_Faith)" title="Paradiso (Dante)">Heaven of Mars</a>, among the other "warriors of the faith".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DLS17_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-DLS17-178">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:D%C3%BCrer_karl_der_grosse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1460" data-file-width="782" decoding="async" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/D%C3%BCrer_karl_der_grosse.jpg/170px-D%C3%BCrer_karl_der_grosse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/D%C3%BCrer_karl_der_grosse.jpg/255px-D%C3%BCrer_karl_der_grosse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/D%C3%BCrer_karl_der_grosse.jpg/340px-D%C3%BCrer_karl_der_grosse.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:D%C3%BCrer_karl_der_grosse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Emperor Charlemagne</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a>, 1511–1513, <a href="/wiki/Germanisches_Nationalmuseum" title="Germanisches Nationalmuseum">Germanisches Nationalmuseum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Charlemagne's capitularies were quoted by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XIV" title="Pope Benedict XIV">Pope Benedict XIV</a> in his apostolic constitution 'Providas' against freemasonry: "For in no way are we able to understand how they can be faithful to us, who have shown themselves unfaithful to God and disobedient to their Priests".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>Charlemagne appears in <i><a href="/wiki/Adelchi" title="Adelchi">Adelchi</a></i>, the second tragedy by Italian writer <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Manzoni" title="Alessandro Manzoni">Alessandro Manzoni</a>, first published in 1822.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBanham1998678_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanham1998678-180">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1867, an <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Charlemagne_(Li%C3%A8ge)" title="Statue of Charlemagne (Liège)">equestrian statue of Charlemagne</a> was made by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jehotte" title="Louis Jehotte">Louis Jehotte</a> and was inaugurated in 1868 on the Boulevard d'Avroy in <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A8ge" title="Liège">Liège</a>. In the niches of the neo-roman <a href="/wiki/Pedestal" title="Pedestal">pedestal</a> are six statues of Charlemagne's ancestors (Sainte Begge, Pépin de Herstal, Charles Martel, Bertrude, Pépin de Landen and Pépin le Bref).
</p><p>The North Wall <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">Frieze</a> in the courtroom of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> depicts Charlemagne as a legal reformer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The city of Aachen has, since 1949, awarded an international prize (called the <i><a href="/wiki/Charlemagne_Prize" title="Charlemagne Prize">Karlspreis</a> der Stadt Aachen</i>) in honour of Charlemagne. It is awarded annually to "personages of merit who have promoted the idea of Western unity by their political, economic and literary endeavours."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[170]</a></sup> Winners of the prize include <a href="/wiki/Richard_von_Coudenhove-Kalergi" title="Richard von Coudenhove-Kalergi">Richard von Coudenhove-Kalergi</a>, the founder of the pan-European movement, <a href="/wiki/Alcide_De_Gasperi" title="Alcide De Gasperi">Alcide De Gasperi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>.
</p><p>In its national anthem, "<a href="/wiki/El_gran_Carlemany" title="El gran Carlemany">El Gran Carlemany</a>", the microstate of <a href="/wiki/Andorra" title="Andorra">Andorra</a> credits Charlemagne with its independence.
</p><p>In 1964, young French singer <a href="/wiki/France_Gall" title="France Gall">France Gall</a> released the hit song "<a href="/wiki/Sacr%C3%A9_Charlemagne" title="Sacré Charlemagne">Sacré Charlemagne</a>" in which the lyrics blame the great king for imposing the burden of compulsory education on French children.
</p><p>Charlemagne is quoted by Dr Henry Jones, Sr. in <i><a href="/wiki/Indiana_Jones_and_the_Last_Crusade" title="Indiana Jones and the Last Crusade">Indiana Jones and the Last Crusade</a></i>. After using his umbrella to induce a flock of seagulls to smash through the glass cockpit of a pursuing German fighter plane, Henry Jones remarks, "I suddenly remembered my Charlemagne: 'Let my armies be the rocks and the trees and the birds in the sky.<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>" Despite the quote's popularity since the movie, there is no evidence that Charlemagne actually said this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2010 episode of <i><a href="/wiki/QI" title="QI">QI</a></i> discussed the mathematics completed by Mark Humphrys that calculated that all modern Europeans are highly likely to share Charlemagne as a common ancestor (see <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">most recent common ancestor</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> featured a weekly column entitled "Charlemagne", focusing generally on European affairs and, more usually and specifically, on the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and its politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>Actor and singer <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Lee" title="Christopher Lee">Christopher Lee</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Symphonic_metal" title="Symphonic metal">symphonic metal</a> <a href="/wiki/Concept_album" title="Concept album">concept album</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Charlemagne:_By_the_Sword_and_the_Cross" title="Charlemagne: By the Sword and the Cross">Charlemagne: By the Sword and the Cross</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michaels_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michaels-186">[174]</a></sup> and its <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">heavy metal</a> follow-up <i><a href="/wiki/Charlemagne:_The_Omens_of_Death" title="Charlemagne: The Omens of Death">Charlemagne: The Omens of Death</a></i> feature the events of Charlemagne's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farrell_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farrell-187">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2014, on the occasion of the 1200th anniversary of Charlemagne's death, public art <i>Mein Karl</i> by <a href="/wiki/Ottmar_H%C3%B6rl" title="Ottmar Hörl">Ottmar Hörl</a> at Katschhof place was installed between city hall and the Aachen cathedral, displaying 500 Charlemagne statues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>Charlemagne features as a playable character in the 2014 <i>Charlemagne</i> expansion for the <a href="/wiki/Grand_strategy_wargame" title="Grand strategy wargame">grand strategy</a> video game <i><a href="/wiki/Crusader_Kings_II" title="Crusader Kings II">Crusader Kings 2</a>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Charlemagne is a playable character in the Mobile/PC Game <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rise_of_Kingdoms&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rise of Kingdoms (page does not exist)">Rise of Kingdoms</a>.
</p><p>In the 2018 video game <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fate/Extella_Link" title="Fate/Extella Link">Fate/Extella Link</a></i>, Charlemagne appears as a Heroic Spirit separated into two Saint Graphs: the adventurous hero Charlemagne, who embodies the fantasy aspect as leader of the Twelve Paladins, and the villain Karl de Große, who embodies the historical aspect as Holy Roman Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2022, Charlemagne featured as a character in an episode of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Family_Histories_Podcast&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Family Histories Podcast (page does not exist)">The Family Histories Podcast</a>, and it references his role as an ancestor of all modern Europeans. He is portrayed here in later life, and is speaking Latin, which is translated by a device. He is returned to 9th Century Aquitaine by the end of the episode after a DNA sample has been extracted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charlemagne&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Alternative birth years for Charlemagne include 742 and 748. There is scholarly debate over this topic, summarised in J. Nelson, Charlemagne (London, 2019), pp. 28-29. See further Karl Ferdinand Werner, <i>Das Geburtsdatum Karls des Großen</i>, in <i>Francia</i> 1, 1973, pp. 115–57 (<a class="external text" href="http://mdzx.bib-bvb.de/francia/Blatt_bsb00016275,00115.html" rel="nofollow">online</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20131117074120/http://mdzx.bib-bvb.de/francia/Blatt_bsb00016275%2C00115.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 17 November 2013 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>);<br/>Matthias Becher: <i>Neue Überlegungen zum Geburtsdatum Karls des Großen</i>, in: <i>Francia</i> 19/1, 1992, pp. 37–60 (<a class="external text" href="http://mdzx.bib-bvb.de/francia/Blatt_bsb00016296,00047.html" rel="nofollow">online</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20131117074146/http://mdzx.bib-bvb.de/francia/Blatt_bsb00016296%2C00047.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 17 November 2013 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Also see: <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20071121072619/http://www.stgeorgegreenville.org/OrthodoxLife/Chapter1/Chap1-7.html" rel="nofollow">The Great Schism – St. George Orthodox Cathedral</a> or <a class="external text" href="http://www.assumptionaz.org/studies_in_orthodoxy/studies_orthodoxy/378" rel="nofollow">The Great Schism – Assumption Greek Orthodox Church</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Through <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_of_Vermandois" title="Beatrice of Vermandois">Beatrice of Vermandois</a>, great-great granddaughter of <a href="/wiki/Pepin_of_Italy" title="Pepin of Italy">Pepin of Italy</a> and grandmother of <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Capet" title="Hugh Capet">Hugh Capet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESII-10.11_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESII-10.11-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hedwiga" title="Hedwiga">Hedwiga</a>, great-great granddaughter of <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Pious" title="Louis the Pious">Louis the Pious</a> and mother of <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Fowler" title="Henry the Fowler">Henry the Fowler</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[12]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Through <a href="/wiki/Albert_II,_Count_of_Namur" title="Albert II, Count of Namur">Albert II, Count of Namur</a>, great-grandson of <a href="/wiki/Louis_IV_of_France" title="Louis IV of France">Louis IV of France</a> and great-great grandfather of <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Blind" title="Henry the Blind">Henry the Blind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[14]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Berengar_II_of_Italy" title="Berengar II of Italy">Berengar II of Italy</a> was a great-great-great grandson of <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Pious" title="Louis the Pious">Louis the Pious</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[15]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Radbot_of_Klettgau" title="Radbot of Klettgau">Radbot of Klettgau</a>, the founder of the House of Habsburg, married Ida of Lorraine, who descended from Charlemagne through both of her parents; from <a href="/wiki/Cunigunda_of_France" title="Cunigunda of France">Cunigunda of France</a> on <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Duke_of_Upper_Lorraine" title="Frederick I, Duke of Upper Lorraine">her father's</a> side and through the Capetians on <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_of_France" title="Beatrice of France">her mother's</a> side.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Frankish_language" title="Old Frankish language">Old Frankish</a>: <i lang="frk">Keril</i>, <i>Karil</i> or <i>Karal</i>, whence <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Karl</i></span> in German and <span title="Dutch-language text"><i lang="nl">Karel</i></span> in Dutch.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See:<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit;word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-free a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Lock-green.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .id-lock-registration a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-registration a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-subscription a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .cs1-ws-icon a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg")right 0.1em center/12px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:none;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;color:#d33}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{color:#d33}.mw-parser-output .cs1-maint{display:none;color:#3a3;margin-left:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right{padding-right:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .citation .mw-selflink{font-weight:inherit}</style><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/215768/France/40279/The-hegemony-of-Neustria" rel="nofollow">"France :: The hegemony of Neustria"</a>. <i>Encyclopædia Britannica</i>. Britannica.com. 24 April 2013<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">14 January</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=France+%3A%3A+The+hegemony+of+Neustria&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclop%C3%A6dia+Britannica&amp;rft.pub=Britannica.com&amp;rft.date=2013-04-24&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.britannica.com%2FEBchecked%2Ftopic%2F215768%2FFrance%2F40279%2FThe-hegemony-of-Neustria&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACharlemagne"></span> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The nature of Himiltrude's relationship to Charlemagne is a matter of dispute. Charlemagne's biographer <a href="/wiki/Einhard" title="Einhard">Einhard</a> calls her a "concubine"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[61]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paulus_Diaconus" title="Paulus Diaconus">Paulus Diaconus</a> speaks of Pippin's birth "before legal marriage",<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A letter by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Stephen_III" title="Pope Stephen III">Pope Stephen III</a> seemingly referring to Charlemagne and his brother Carloman as being already married (to Himiltrude and <a href="/wiki/Gerberga,_wife_of_Carloman_I" title="Gerberga, wife of Carloman I">Gerberga</a>), and advising them not to dismiss their wives has led many historians to believe that Himiltrude and Charlemagne were legally married.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, the words employed by the pope could also simply mean that there had only been a promise of marriage. The acts of Saint <a href="/wiki/Adalard_of_Corbie" title="Adalard of Corbie">Adalard of Corbie</a> supports this hypothesis, for the monastic vocation of that Saint is described as due to the scruple he had regarding Charlemagne's dismissal of Princess <a href="/wiki/Desiderata_of_the_Lombards" title="Desiderata of the Lombards">Desiderata of the Lombards</a> which occurred before any consummation of the marriage and possibly before any religious ceremony. (It is unclear whether the marriage ever took place or if  Desiderata only received the homage of the nobility in accordance with her planned future position of Queen of the Franks.) If Saint Adalard was scandalised by this dismissal, it is highly unlikely he would have been unfazed about Himiltrude's dismissal, had she truly been married to Charlemagne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[62]</a></sup> Historians have interpreted the information in different ways. Some, such as <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Rich%C3%A9" title="Pierre Riché">Pierre Riché</a>, follow Einhard in describing Himiltrude as a concubine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[63]</a></sup> Others, Dieter Hägemann for example, consider Himiltrude a wife in the full sense.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Still others subscribe to the idea that the relationship between the two was "something more than concubinage, less than marriage" and describe it as a <a href="/wiki/Friedelehe" title="Friedelehe">Friedelehe</a>, a supposed form of marriage unrecognized by the Church and easily dissolvable. This form of relationship is often seen in a conflict between Christian marriage and more flexible Germanic concepts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From 781 Adrian began dating papal documents by the years of Charlemagne's reign, instead of the reign of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Emperor" title="Byzantine Emperor">Byzantine Emperor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[65]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was during this visit to Rome that Charlemagne met <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcuin_of_York" title="Alcuin of York">Alcuin of York</a> and invited him to join his court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsimov1968168_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsimov1968168-77">[66]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-Will_Durant_Chronique-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Will_Durant_Chronique_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Will_Durant_Chronique_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Durant, Will. <a class="external text" href="http://www.chronique.com/Library/MedHistory/charlemagne.htm" rel="nofollow">"King Charlemagne."</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20111224205023/http://www.chronique.com/Library/MedHistory/charlemagne.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 24 December 2011 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. <li id="cite_note-ESII-10.11-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ESII-10.11_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Detlev Schwennicke, <i>Europäische Stammtafeln: Stammtafeln zur Geschichte der Europäischen Staaten</i>, Neue Folge, Band II (Marburg, Germany: J. <li id="cite_note-172"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-172">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1" id="CITEREFShahanMacpherson1908">Shahan, Thomas; Macpherson, Ewan (1908). <a class="external text" href="http://www.newadvent.org/cathen/03610c.htm" rel="nofollow">"Charlemagne"</a>. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Catholic_Encyclopedia" title="The Catholic Encyclopedia">The Catholic Encyclopedia</a></i>. <li id="cite_note-176"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-176">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFFraley1993">Fraley, Michael (1993). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=pnxdCwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA44" rel="nofollow"><i>Arthur: King of Britain</i></a>. Caliber Comics. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1626657984" title="Special:BookSources/978-1626657984"><bdi>978-1626657984</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Arthur%3A+King+of+Britain&amp;rft.pub=Caliber+Comics&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.isbn=978-1626657984&amp;rft.aulast=Fraley&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DpnxdCwAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA44&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACharlemagne"></span><br/>"To anyone familiar with the early Medieval Period of European history, Geoffrey's story begins to sound familiar here. It should. It seems to be based, in large part, on the historical adventures of Charlemagne, the Frankish King of the Ninth Century, who eventually became Emperor. Looking at Charlemagne's life and personality, it becomes clear that he and Geoffrey's Arthur are practically twins."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-177"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-177">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.mementomedievalia.com/charlemagne-king-arthur-and-contested-national-identity-in-english-romances/" rel="nofollow">Charlemagne, King Arthur and Contested National Identity in English Romances</a><br/> Modelling his narrative on earlier Middle English texts, the English AMA-poet, appropriates aspects of the historical reality of Charlemagne and refashions them to fit Arthur, creating a hero that the English can claim as their own.</span>

<li id="cite_note-Michaels-186"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Michaels_186-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFMichaels2010">Michaels, Sean (5 January 2010). <a class="external text" href="https://www.theguardian.com/music/2010/jan/05/christopher-lee-symphonic-metal-album" rel="nofollow">"Christopher Lee to release 'symphonic metal' album, The man who played Dracula and Saruman is to tell the story of Charlemagne, the first Holy Roman Emperor, through the universal language of metal"</a>. <i>The Guardian</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">1 January</span> 2013</span>. <q>The man who played Dracula, Saruman and the Man with the Golden Gun is now to portray Charlemagne—through the medium of song.
